aspmc: New frontiers of algebraic answer set counting

A B S T R A C T
In the last decade, there has been increasing interest in extensions of answer set programming
(ASP) that cater for quantitative information such as weights or probabilities. A wide range of
quantitative reasoning tasks for ASP and logic programming, among them probabilistic inference
and parameter learning in the neuro-symbolic setting, can be expressed as algebraic answer set
counting (AASC) tasks, i.e., weighted model counting for ASP with weights calculated over
some semiring, which makes eпђГcient solvers for AASC desirable. In this article, we present
aspmc, a new solver for AASC that pushes the limits of eпђГcient solvability. Notably, aspmc
provides improved performance compared to the state of the art in probabilistic inference by
exploiting three insights gained from thorough theoretical investigations in our work. Namely,
we consider the knowledge compilation step in the AASC pipeline, where the underlying logical
theory speciпђБed by the answer set program is converted into a tractable circuit representation,
on which AASC is feasible in polynomial time. First, we provide a detailed comparison of
diпђАerent approaches to knowledge compilation for programs, revealing that translation to
propositional formulas followed by compilation to sd-DNNF seems favorable. Second, we study
how the translation to propositional formulas should proceed to result in eпђГcient compilation.
This leads to the second and third insight, namely a novel way of breaking the positive
cyclic dependencies in a program, called TоИЉ -Unfolding, and an improvement to the Clark
Completion, the procedure used to transform programs without positive cyclic dependencies into
propositional formulas. Both improvements are tailored towards eпђГcient knowledge compilation.
Our empirical evaluation reveals that while all three advancements contribute to the success of
aspmc, TоИЉ -Unfolding improves performance signiпђБcantly by allowing us to handle cyclic instances
better.

1. Introduction
In the last decades but especially in recent years, there has been an increasing interest in reasoning frameworks that combine
logical and numerical aspects, in order to deal with both qualitative and quantitative uncertainty. This is of particular importance for
the integration of machine learning and knowledge representation [1вАУ3], probabilistic planning [4], bio informatics [5,6], natural
language processing [7], etc.
The trend of tackling these problems is especially visible in the context of logic programming (LP) [8] and answer set programming (ASP) [9]. There, diпђАerent approaches for probabilistic reasoning were introduced, among them LPMLN [10], P-log [11],

T. Eiter, M. Hecher and R. Kiesel

ProbLog [12], PITA [13], CP-logic [14], SMProbLog [15], and many others [16вАУ18]. Neuro-symbolic reasoning, which builds on top of
probabilistic inference for a пђБxed distribution, is explored by NeurASP [19], SLASH [20], and DeepProbLog [1]. The idea is natural:
the semantics for a given set of weights is that of one of the previous probabilistic extensions of LP/ASP but instead of assuming
the weights to be given, we learn (to predict) them. Apart from this, also model counting [21,22], optimization [23,24], and other
quantitative extensions [25вАУ28] enable reasoning with ASP on problems that go beyond the solution of yes/no questions.
Motivation The plethora of reasoning tasks and settings raises the need to develop a range of algorithms and implementations. This
motivates us to consider quantitative reasoning over the set of models in ASP from a general and unifying perspective. Fortunately,
while many reasoning tasks in the formalisms above are vastly diпђАerent at пђБrst glance, it was shown that they can be approached
in a uniform manner by considering them from an algebraic point of view [29вАУ32]. SpeciпђБcally, in the Semiring Paradigm one uses
the abstract algebraic structure of a semiring оИЊ = (рЭСЕ, вКХ, вКЧ, рЭСТвКХ , рЭСТвКЧ ) to capture a multitude of diпђАerent quantitative semantics by
expressions over a set рЭСЕ of elements using an вАЬadditionвАЭ вКХ and a вАЬmultiplicationвАЭ вКЧ that satisfy some axiomes, such as associativity
and commutativity. For example, the probabilistic semiring оИЉ = ([0, 1], +, вЛЕ, 0, 1) enables probabilistic inference; the tropical semiring
оИЊmax,+ = (оИЊ вИ™ {вИТвИЮ}, max, +, вИТвИЮ, 0) corresponds to optimization by choosing the solution with the maximal weight; and EisnerвАЩs [33]
gradient semiring allows for parameter learning. By performing algebraic answer set counting (AASC), which is to sum up the (semiring)
values associated with each answer set of a given program, we then immediately obtain a solution to the original quantitative
reasoning task at hand.
Main aim However, solving AASC eпђГciently poses a challenge. It is well-known that quantitative reasoning may be (much) harder
than qualitative reasoning, e.g., lifting in ASP problems from NP-completeness to PNP -completeness or to #P-hardness (see [34] and
references therein). This calls for the need of methods and techniques in order to advance the performance of AASC in practice,
which is the main goal of our work.
Existing work Since there already exists a wide range of implementations and diverse approaches [35,36,13,37,38,21,39,40], it
is suggestive to improve upon an existing idea. However, there is no consensus regarding which evaluation strategy is generally
favorable. Thus, it is unclear which approach to choose as the basis of our work. SpeciпђБcally, in enumeration-based approaches [19,
38,37], one enumerates all the answer sets one after the other and sums up their weights one by one. While the idea is simple, it works
well for вАЬfewвАЭ (less than a billion) answer sets. However, it does not scale when there are many answer sets, even for structurally
very simple programs. In contrast, knowledge compilation-based approaches [36,40,13] are more sophisticated and produce a so called
tractable circuit representation of a given program. They represent the answer sets of a program, using knowledge compilation [41,42],
in a manner that can be vastly more succinct than an explicit representation of all the answer sets while still allowing one to perform
AASC using linearly many semiring operations in the size of the resulting circuit [30]. Apart from that, there is also the option to
perform dynamic programming [21] when the treewidth of the program is low enough. Here, treewidth is a parameter that intuitively
gives an upper-bound on how complex the structure of a program (or more generally a graph) is by measuring how вАЬtreelikeвАЭ the
shape of rule dependencies is, which determines how expensive it is to decompose the problem into smaller ones in a recursive
manner.
However, there are not only diпђАerent basic approaches. For knowledge compilation, for instance, there are diпђАerent classes of
tractable circuits that one can compile to; we may compile some circuits either in a вАЬbottom upвАЭ [43,44] or in a вАЬtop downвАЭ [45вАУ47]
manner; and we may either compile the program directly or convert it before compilation into a propositional formula in Conjunctive
Normal Form (CNF).
Approach and methods In a пђБrst step, we therefore investigate diпђАerent strategies in depth from a theoretical perspective using
recent results from the пђБeld of parameterized complexity [48вАУ52]. While these results do not give us general lower bounds for
complexity, they do give us upper bounds in terms of structural parameters, such as treewidth or pathwidth, which we can use to
derive performance guarantees for the considered strategies. Based on this analysis, we conclude that the most promising strategy is to
пђБrst translate programs into CNFs and compile them using one of the highly advanced tools from the SAT community [46,45,53,47].
Naturally, this CNF translation should be chosen in such a manner that it leads to good performance of knowledge compilation.
From the previous theoretical analysis, we know that small CNFs of low treewidth allow for good worst case guarantees. In fact,
there is recent empirical evidence that shows that explicitly exploiting the treewidth of CNFs during knowledge compilation can lead
to signiпђБcantly improved performance [47]. Apart from the size and treewidth, we assume that it is beneпђБcial if the CNF encoding
is not very вАЬsemantically complexвАЭ such that intuitively the knowledge compiler can easily identify parts of the search space that
it may discard. This last measure is rather imprecise; nonetheless, we try to use it by drawing intuition from known rules of thumb
from CNF encodings for SAT.
The translation of answer set programs to CNFs usually proceeds in two steps. First the cyclic positive dependencies in the
program need to be taken care of in cycle breaking. Only then, in the second step, ClarkвАЩs Completion [54] is guaranteed to produce a
CNF that correctly captures the semantics of the program. For ClarkвАЩs Completion itself, we consider it rather unlikely that there is a
way to signiпђБcantly reduce the size or semantic complexity of the resulting CNF. However, previous work by Hecher [55] has shown
that we can modify the completion to limit the treewidth increase compared to the primal treewidth of the program to a low constant
factor. We improve this result to use the incidence treewidth of the program [22], which provides bounds that are better or equal.
As for cycle breaking, a wide range of approaches exists [56вАУ59,55], and so we again need a more in-depth analysis. Some
of the approaches do not preserve answer sets in a bijective fashion [59,55] and thus have to be discarded, while we examine
2

Artificial Intelligence 330 (2024) 104109

T. Eiter, M. Hecher and R. Kiesel

encoding properties of the rest. Here, we see that the вАЬsemantic complexityвАЭ of Janhunen and Niemel√§вАЩs work [56] may due to
the use of binary counters prove to be problematic, but the asymptotic guarantees for CNF size and treewidth are far better than
those from Mantadelis and JanssensвАЩs [58] work. Nevertheless, previous empirical results showed that the asymptotic guarantees
do not necessarily translate to better performance, which is presumably due to high constant factors hidden by the Big-O notation
and the high вАЬsemantic complexityвАЭ [36]. We thus introduce a novel strategy for cycle breaking called TоИЉ -Unfolding, where we aim
to reach a middle ground between the previous approaches. That is, we obtain similarly low вАЬsemantic complexityвАЭ and constant
factors as Mantadelis and Janssens [58] but only moderately higher asymptotic size and treewidth guarantees than Janhunen and
Niemel√§ [56]. In order to achieve this, we resort not only to the number of atomic formulas that are involved in cyclic dependencies,
but we also provide a novel, more sensitive measure of the cyclicity of the positive dependencies. This measure, which we call the
component-boosted backdoor size,1 is related to the notion of backdoor size into acyclicity [60], which intuitively asks how many atoms
we must remove to make the (dependency) graph acyclic.
In addition to our theoretical advancements, we implemented our пђБndings in the open source AASC solver aspmc and use it
to validate that our theoretical considerations indeed result in an increased performance in practice. To this end, we conducted a
thorough empirical evaluation on typical benchmarks from the probabilistic logic programming community, which conпђБrms previous
expectations regarding the CNF sizes and treewidths as well as the performance increase.
Main contributions

Summing up, we make in this work brieпђВy the following main contributions:

1. Our analysis of diпђАerent approaches to the knowledge compilation step in AASC highlights possible strengths and weaknesses
allowing for a more informed choice of the knowledge compilation method in the AASC pipeline and provide explanations as to
why some strategies may work better than others.
2. We introduce an improved version of ClarkвАЩs Completion [54] that can, in some cases, provide even better treewidth guarantees
than HecherвАЩs [55] previous modiпђБcation with minimal overhead otherwise.
3. By employing a more пђБne-grained measure of cyclicity in answer set programs, we deпђБne TоИЉ -Unfolding, a new approach for
cycle breaking that has favorable properties when it comes to knowledge compilation.
4. We provide an open source implementation of our improved and specialized algorithms for ClarkвАЩs Completion and cycle breaking
in the AASC solver aspmc. In addition, aspmc enables the use of recent advancements in knowledge compilation by integrating
state of the art implementations [47,46,45,53].
5. We report on an extensive empirical evaluation using our implementation. The results reveals that all three improvements provide
signiпђБcant performance beneпђБts:
вАҐ our modiпђБcation to ClarkвАЩs Completion outperforms previous versions in most cases;
вАҐ TоИЉ -Unfolding, results in small CNF encodings with a moderate increase in the treewidth;
вАҐ the overall performance is further boosted by employing state of the art tools for knowledge compilation.
Additional contributions of the extended version Some of the results in this article were presented, in preliminary form, at the KR 2021
conference [61] and the PLP 2021 workshop [62]. The additional contributions of this work, are twofold. First, we signiпђБcantly
improved the implementation. Here, we on the one hand improved eпђГciency, by incorporating additional specialized tools, e.g., to
compute approximations of our measure of cyclicity [63] and for the knowledge compilation step [47,53]. On the other hand, we
enabled inference for the full class of probabilistic logic programs, with annotated disjunctions. Second, we incorporated an in-depth
analysis of the diпђАerent approaches to AASC. Here, we not only added a wide ranges of theoretical tractability guarantees but also
signiпђБcantly extended our practical evaluation by comparing a wide range of internal conпђБgurations and by including more solvers
and benchmarks to the general comparison to the state of the art.
Relation to AI and usage of our results Algebraic operations and counting solutions are fundamental tasks in mathematics and
computer science [64вАУ66], with applications to areas such as mathematical combinatorics [67], learning [68,10], neuro-symbolic
reasoning [38,69], and probabilistic inference [70,15,11]. Given this strong basis, AI researchers and developers can beneпђБt from the
results of our work in diпђАerent regards. On the foundational side, researchers can acquire new insights in properties of knowledge
compilation methods. This is particularly interesting also for propositional formulas that encode properties like transitive closure,
acyclicity, or are based on cyclic derivations, e.g., reachability in a graphical network. Our work also implies how to overcome
these challenging barriers without destroying structural properties that are essential for eпђГciently employing algebraic (semiring)
operations. On the practical side, researchers can directly take advantage of our new notions and methods, in particular, the improved
ClarkвАЩs Completion, component-boosted backdoors, and the TоИЉ -Unfolding for developing novel reasoning engines or advancing
existing engines. Furthermore, on the application side, developers may employ aspmc as a back-end solver or library for easy oпђА-theshelf evaluation. Indeed, our excessive empirical evaluation underlines the strengths of aspmc, paving the way to future applications
in systems utilizing counting, quantitative reasoning, or neurosymbolic learning and reasoning.

Organization The remainder of this article is organized as follows. We пђБrst provide the necessary preliminaries in Section 2 and
then introduce AASC in Section 3. After this, we shortly go over some instantiations of AASC highlighting its broad applicability for
quantitative inference in Section 4, before directing our attention to AASC evaluation in Section 5, where we give special attention to
diпђАerent approaches for the knowledge compilation step. We пђБnish the theoretical considerations with a study of ClarkвАЩs Completion
and cycle breakings, in Section 6 and Section 7, respectively. Based on this, we outline the practical realization in our solver aspmc,
in Section 8, which we evaluate empirically, in Section 9. Finally, we conclude and highlight possible further points of attack for
improved performance in Section 10.
2. Preliminaries
We begin by giving the background on ASP, graph representations of programs, their associated structural parameters, and the
algebraic structure of semirings.
2.1. Logic programming
A (normal) answer set program ќ† is a пђБnite set of rules рЭСЯ of the form

рЭСО вЖР рЭСП1 , вА¶ , рЭСПрЭСЪ , not рЭСР1 , вА¶ , not рЭСРрЭСЫ ,
where рЭСО and all рЭСПрЭСЧ , рЭСРрЭСШ are propositional atoms. Given such a rule рЭСЯ, we let

рЭРї(рЭСЯ) = рЭСО,

рЭРµ + (рЭСЯ) = {рЭСП1 , вА¶ , рЭСПрЭСЫ },

рЭРµ вИТ (рЭСЯ) = {рЭСР1 , вА¶ , рЭСРрЭСЫ },

рЭРµ(рЭСЯ) = {рЭСП1 , вА¶ , рЭСПрЭСЫ , not рЭСР1 , вА¶ , not рЭСРрЭСЫ }.

We slightly abuse notation and write

for

where вК• is a propositional atom that otherwise does not occur in ќ†.
Furthermore, we allow choice rules {рЭСО} вЖР рЭРµ + (рЭСЯ), рЭРµ вИТ (рЭСЯ) as a shorthand for the two rules рЭСО вЖР рЭРµ + (рЭСЯ), рЭРµ вИТ (рЭСЯ), not рЭСЫрЭСО and рЭСЫрЭСО вЖР
+
рЭРµ (рЭСЯ), рЭРµ вИТ (рЭСЯ), not рЭСО, where рЭСЫрЭСО is a fresh propositional atom. We denote by оИ≠(ќ†) the set of propositional atoms that occur in ќ†.
An interpretation, denoted оИµ , is a subset of оИ≠(ќ†); it satisпђБes an atom рЭСО вИИ оИ≠(ќ†) (resp. not рЭСО for рЭСО вИИ оИ≠(ќ†)), written оИµ вКІ рЭСО (resp.

of answer sets of a program ќ† by оИ≠оИњ(ќ†).
As usual the schematic rules with variables рЭСЛ, рЭСМ , вА¶ are implicitly universally quantiпђБed and their semantics is given by grounding

Example 1 (Smokers). We consider the smokers program, which is a standard example from probabilistic logic programming [12].

{stress(рЭСЛ)} вЖР person(рЭСЛ)
smokes(рЭСЛ) вЖР stress(рЭСЛ)

This encodes that for each person it is randomly determined whether they are stressed. Stressed persons smoke may inпђВuence friends,
which is again random, to also smoke. We shall abbreviate stress(.) and smokes(.) by st(.) and sm(.), respectively.
2.2. Propositional logic
We aim to translate programs into formulas of propositional logic.
We use propositional formulas in Conjunctive Normal Form (CNF). A CNF оИѓ , deпђБned for a set рЭСЙ of variables, is a пђБnite conjunction
of clauses рЭРґрЭСЦ , where each clause consists of a пђБnite disjunction of literals рЭСЩ вИИ {рЭС£, ¬ђрЭС£} for some рЭС£ вИИ рЭСЙ .
We use the standard satisfaction relation and call an interpretation оИµ вКЖ рЭСЙ that satisпђБes a formula a model.

2.3. Graphs and digraphs
Especially in Section 7.4 we will consider graphs and digraphs, using the following notation. The vertex- and edge-set of a
(di)graph рЭРЇ = (рЭСЙ , рЭРЄ) is denoted by рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) and рЭРЄ(рЭРЇ), respectively. We denote the undirected edge between vertices рЭСО, рЭСП вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) of a
graph рЭРЇ by {рЭСО, рЭСП}, whereas for the directed edge from рЭСО to рЭСП of a digraph, we use the notation (рЭСО, рЭСП). For рЭСЙ вКЖ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) we let рЭРЇ[рЭСЙ ]
be the (di)graph obtained by removing all vertices not in рЭСЙ from рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) (i.e. рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ[рЭСЙ ]) = рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) вИ© рЭСЙ ) and removing all edges which
use a vertex not in рЭСЙ (i.e. рЭРЄ(рЭРЇ[рЭСЙ ]) = рЭРЄ(рЭСЙ ) вИ© рЭСЙ √Ч рЭСЙ ). Further, we deпђБne рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЙ as рЭРЇ[рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) вІµ рЭСЙ ]. The subgraph рЭРґ = рЭРЇ[рЭСЙ ] is strongly
connected if every vertex in рЭРґ is reachable from any other vertex in рЭРґ . We denote by SCC(рЭРЇ) the set of strongly connected components
(SCC) of рЭРЇ , which are strongly connected subgraphs рЭРЇ[рЭСЙ ], where рЭСЙ is subset-maximal.
The (positive) dependency graph DEP(ќ†) of a program ќ† is the digraph рЭРЇ with рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) = оИ≠(ќ†) and (рЭСП, рЭСО) вИИ рЭРЄ(рЭРЇ) if there is a rule
рЭСЯ вИИ ќ† such that рЭСО вИИ рЭРї(рЭСЯ) and рЭСП вИИ рЭРµ + (рЭСЯ). The primal graph PRIM(ќ†) of ќ† is the graph рЭРЇ with рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) = оИ≠(ќ†) and {рЭС•, рЭС¶} вИИ рЭРЄ(рЭРЇ) if there
is a rule рЭСЯ вИИ ќ† such that рЭС•, рЭС¶ вИИ {рЭРї(рЭСЯ)} вИ™ рЭРµ + (рЭСЯ) вИ™ рЭРµ вИТ (рЭСЯ). The incidence graph INC(ќ†) of ќ† is the graph рЭРЇ with рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) = оИ≠(ќ†) вИ™ ќ† and
{рЭСО, рЭСЯ} вИИ рЭРЄ(рЭРЇ) if there is a rule рЭСЯ вИИ ќ† such that рЭСО вИИ {рЭРї(рЭСЯ)} вИ™ рЭРµ + (рЭСЯ) вИ™ рЭРµ вИТ (рЭСЯ).
Intuitively, all three graphs represent that the truth values of atoms (and rules in the case of the incidence graph) connected via an
edge directly depend on one another. More speciпђБcally, the dependency graph is related to whether an atom could positively occur
on a derivation of another one and the primal graph is related to whether satisfaction of one atom may directly limit the possible
truth values of another atom in models of a program. The incidence graph is similar to the primal graph but allows for a more пђБne
grained analysis by including not only atoms but also rules.

Next, we recall the deпђБnition of treewidth.
DeпђБnition 1 (Tree Decomposition, Treewidth). A tree decomposition of a graph рЭРЇ is a pair (рЭСЗ , рЭЬТ), where рЭСЗ is a tree and рЭЬТ is a labeling
of рЭСЙ (рЭСЗ ) by subsets of рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) s.t.
вАҐ for all nodes рЭС£ вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) there is рЭС° вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭСЗ ) s.t. рЭС£ вИИ рЭЬТ(рЭС°);
вАҐ for every edge {рЭС£1 , рЭС£2 } вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭРЄ) there exists рЭС° вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭСЗ ) s.t. рЭС£1 , рЭС£2 вИИ рЭЬТ(рЭС°);
вАҐ for all nodes рЭС£ вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) the set of nodes {рЭС° вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭСЗ ) вИ£ рЭС£ вИИ рЭЬТ(рЭС°)} forms a (connected) subtree of рЭСЗ .
The width of (рЭСЗ , рЭЬТ) is maxрЭС°вИИрЭСЙ вА≤ |рЭЬТ(рЭС°)| вИТ 1. The treewidth of a graph is the minimal width of any of its tree decompositions.
Intuitively, treewidth is a measure of the distance of a graph from a tree. It is motivated by the fact that many computationally
hard problems are tractable on trees. Correspondingly, trees are the only graphs that have treewidth 1. Given low treewidth it is
then often possible to generalize tractability results by decomposing problems recursively into smaller subproblems using a tree
decomposition witnessing the low width.
A more restricted parameter than treewidth is pathwidth.
DeпђБnition 2 (Path Decomposition, Pathwidth). Let рЭРЇ be a graph. Then, a path decomposition is a tree decomposition (рЭСЗ , рЭЬТ), where рЭСЗ
is a path.
The pathwidth of a graph is the minimal width of any of its path decompositions.
Pathwidth is more restricted than treewidth but has similar properties, i.e. it allows for the generalization of tractability results
on graphs that are paths. Notably, when the treewidth of an рЭСЫ-vertex graph рЭРЇ is рЭСШ, then the pathwidth of рЭРЇ is in оИї(рЭСШ вЛЕ log(рЭСЫ)) [73].
Then, the primal (resp. incidence) treewidth of a program is the treewidth of its primal (resp. incidence) graph. The same applies
to pathwidth.
Example 3 (contвАЩd). The treewidth of the graph in Fig. 1b is 2, as its tree decomposition in Fig. 2 which has width 2 shows. A smaller
width is not possible here, since the graph contains a clique over three vertices (e.g. sm(1), sm(2), sm(3)). The treewidth of the graph
in Fig. 1c is also 2, since it is not a tree as the cycle рЭСЯ2 вЖТ sm(3) вЖТ рЭСЯ6 вЖТ sm(2) вЖТ рЭСЯ4 вЖТ sm(1) вЖТ рЭСЯ2 shows.
Apart from the logical aspects speciпђБed using the program, we also need semirings to specify the quantitative aspects.
2.4. Semirings
A semiring оИЊ = (рЭСЕ, вКХ, вКЧ, рЭСТвКХ , рЭСТвКЧ ) consists of a non-empty set рЭСЕ equipped with two binary operations вКХ and вКЧ, called addition
and multiplication, where
вАҐ (рЭСЕ, вКХ) is a commutative monoid with identity element рЭСТвКХ ,
вАҐ (рЭСЕ, вКЧ) is a monoid with identity element рЭСТвКЧ ,
вАҐ multiplication left and right distributes over addition, and
вАҐ рЭСТвКХ annihilates рЭСЕ, i.e. вИАрЭСЯ вИИ рЭСЕ вИґ рЭСЯвКЧрЭСТвКХ = рЭСТвКХ = рЭСТвКХ вКЧрЭСЯ.
A semiring is commutative, if (рЭСЕ, вКЧ) is commutative, and is idempotent, if вИАрЭСЯ вИИ рЭСЕ вИґ рЭСЯвКХрЭСЯ = рЭСЯ.
In the following, we restrict ourselves to commutative semirings. Some examples of well-known semirings are
вАҐ рЭФљ = (рЭФљ , +, вЛЕ, 0, 1), for рЭФљ вИИ {вДХ, вД§, вДЪ, вДЭ} the semiring of the numbers in рЭФљ with addition and multiplication,
вАҐ оИЊmax = (вДХ вИ™ {вИТвИЮ}, max, +, вИТвИЮ, 0), the max-plus semiring,
вАҐ рЭФє = ({0, 1}, вИ®, вИІ, 0, 1), the Boolean semiring,
вАҐ оИЉ = ([0, 1], +, вЛЕ, 0, 1), the probability semiring.
6

Artificial Intelligence 330 (2024) 104109

T. Eiter, M. Hecher and R. Kiesel

All of the aforementioned semirings are commutative.
For a more comprehensive list of semirings and applications see [30].
3. Algebraic answer set counting
We now introduce ASP with algebraic measures as a basic formalism for Algebraic Answer Set Counting (AASC). Technically,
ASP with algebraic measures extends ASP with capabilities for quantitative reasoning over the set of answer sets such that algebraic
Prolog, ProbLog, LPMLN and P-log can all be expressed.
We use a variant of weighted logics [74] restricted to propositional formulas.

Example 5 (contвАЩd). Using algebraic measures, we perform probabilistic reasoning. We combine the weighted formula рЭЫЉ , which

Then, the answer set оИµ = {st(1), sm(1)} has weight рЭЬЗрЭС†рЭСЪ (оИµ) = 0.4 вЛЕ 0.62 вЛЕ 0.73 .
The query рЭЬЗ(sm(1)) corresponds to the probability that sm(1) holds. To evaluate it, we need to perform AASC, i.e. sum up the
probabilities of all answer sets s.t. sm(1) holds. Since ќ†рЭС†рЭСЪ has 26 answer sets out of which 25 + 23 + 2 include sm(1), we refrain from
computing рЭЬЗ(sm(1)) na√ѓvely by considering all the answer sets separately. Instead, we consider the following three disjoint cases in
which sm(1) holds:
1. st(1) is true and the other probabilistic atoms take an arbitrary truth value;
7

Artificial Intelligence 330 (2024) 104109

T. Eiter, M. Hecher and R. Kiesel

2. st(1) is false, inf (3, 1) and st(3) are true and the other probabilistic atoms take an arbitrary truth value;
3. st(1) and st(3) are false, inf(3, 1), inf (2, 3) and st(2) are true, and inf(1, 2) takes an arbitrary truth value.
These three cases are exclusive, i.e. no answer set falls in any two of the cases, and furthermore, they cover all the answer sets
in which sm(1) is true. Thus, we can compute the probability of sm(1) as a sum of the probabilities of the three cases. These are
0.4, 0.6 вИЧ 0.3 вИЧ 0.4(= 0.072) and 0.6 вИЧ 0.6 вИЧ 0.3 вИЧ 0.3 вИЧ 0.4(= 0.01296), respectively, since it is enough to take the product of the
probabilities of the atoms whose truth values we assert as the other atoms that are left arbitrary only contribute a factor of 1. Thus,
рЭЬЗ(sm(1)) = 0.48496.
Following the conventions of [75], we also introduce factorized measures.
DeпђБnition 5 (Factorized Measure). Let рЭЬЗ = вЯ®ќ†, рЭЫЉ, оИЊвЯ© be an algebraic measure and рЭРє вКЖ оИ≠(ќ†). Then, рЭЬЗ is factorized w.r.t. рЭРє , if there is

The diпђАerence between factorized and non-factorized measures is intuitively how complicated the weight function is. The former
must be expressible as a product of weights of literals that are true in a given interpretation, whereas the latter allow complex
arithmetic expressions using both the addition and multiplication of weights in dependence on the true literals in the interpretation.
The motivation behind considering factorized measures is that current frameworks, such as ProbLog and algebraic Prolog only
use factorized measures. This raises the question of whether this covers everything we need.
Example 6 (contвАЩd). The measure рЭЬЗрЭС†рЭСЪ is factorized over st(рЭСЦ), рЭСЦ = 1, 2, 3 and inf(рЭСЦ, рЭСЧ), рЭСЦ + 1 вЙ° рЭСЧ mod 3, by letting рЭЫљ(st(рЭСЦ)) = 0.4, рЭЫљ(¬ђst(рЭСЦ)) =

Not every measure is factorized however.

4. Applications of AASC
We covered the introductory theory necessary when dealing with AASC and algebraic measures. In the following, we shortly
reiterate some of the prominent applications of AASC via instantiation with пђБxed semirings.
The idea behind AASC is ubiquitous in quantitative reasoning: one takes a Boolean logic and turns it into a weighted logic, by
replacing conjunctions by multiplications and disjunctions by additions over a semiring. Other such semiring reasoning frameworks
are for example Semiring-based Constraint Satisfaction Problems [76], provenance semantics for datalog [77], and Algebraic Model
Counting (AMC) [30], to name only a few. Due to this, a wide range of applications of the semiring semantics are already known.
Kimmig et al. [30] give an extensive list of diпђАerent reasoning tasks that can be performed using the semiring semantics and discuss
the appropriate semirings for it.
We therefore do not extensively list all the diпђАerent possibilities here but focus on some that relate to existing extensions of ASP
and, thus, have higher relevance for our setting. Namely, we focus on two diпђАerent classes of problems and touch upon a third. First,
we consider problems that are #P-hard, i.e. at least as hard as counting the number of satisfying assignments of a propositional
formula. For these problems knowledge compilation is a well-established and competitive technique [46,45]. Second, when we work
over idempotent semirings, algebraic measure evaluation is often equivalent to O PT P-hard problems. Here, at least in the area of
Weighted MaxSAT, a prototypical O PT P-hard problem, knowledge compilation does not seem to be a good strategy, given that none
of the solvers of the recent MaxSAT Evaluation [78] is knowledge compilation based. Thus, while we relate AASC to O PT P-hard
problems here and cover their solution also with our implementation (which works for general commutative semirings), we do not
expect performance improvements compared to specialized tools such as clingo [79] here.
We note that there are relevant problems that are in a complexity class above #P. Recent work [32] showed that many such
problems can be solved by AASC over two semirings but not over one of them. Here, the problems are typically harder than AASC
over one semiring, since they remain NP-hard on the usual tractable circuit representations [80]. Note that, as with the O PT P-hard
problems, an implementation that works for general semirings is available but not further considered here, since we focus on the
evaluation of problems that are #P-hard but solvable on typical tractable circuit representations. For details we refer the interested
reader to [32].
4.1. #P-hard problems
Probabilistic reasoning The most common application that involves weighted model counting over the answer sets, in the typical
sense, is probabilistic inference as in ProbLog, LPMLN , or P-log. The speciпђБcation and semantics of probabilistic programs as well as
the allowed class of programs varies between the languages. In fact, there is also Credal Semantics for probabilistic programs [81],
which we however leave untreated in this work.
The main diпђАerence between the treated languages is that ProbLog uses SatoвАЩs distribution semantics [82], which assumes that
the only non-determinism in the program is due to a set of probabilistic facts and that the program has no constraints. This means
9

Artificial Intelligence 330 (2024) 104109

T. Eiter, M. Hecher and R. Kiesel

that any given truth assignment to the probabilistic facts can be uniquely extended to a stable model of the program. The beneпђБt of
this restriction is that the measure speciпђБed by the program directly satisпђБes the axioms of a probability distribution, e.g. the weights
of all answer sets add up to one. On the other hand, LPMLN and P-log do not impose such a restriction, thus allowing for a broader
class of programs. Here, in order to obtain a probability measure, the probability of an answer set is not its weight but its normalized
weight. That is, its weight is divided by the sum of the weights of all answer sets.
Apart from that, LPMLN features another property. Namely, while ProbLog and P-log only allow for weights of atoms or rules that
are between 0 and 1, LPMLN allows for any real number as a weight.
For probabilistic inference by means of AASC, both of these diпђАerences are not signiпђБcant. We can perform probabilistic inference
in any of the three languages by evaluating an algebraic measure over an answer set program. For ProbLog programs, it is suпђГcient
to evaluate рЭЬЗ(рЭСО) for the algebraic measure рЭЬЗ corresponding to the program to obtain the probability that the query atom рЭСО is true
according to the program. In the ProbLog case, we can use the probabilistic semiring оИЉ since only weights in [0, 1] occur. For LPMLN
and P-log programs, we can compute the probability of a query atom рЭСО by evaluating рЭЬЗ(рЭСО) and рЭЬЗ(not рЭСО) over the semiring вДЭ of the
real numbers. Then, рЭСГ рЭСЯ(рЭСО), the probability of рЭСО, is рЭЬЗ(рЭСО)вИХрЭЬЗ(рЭСО)+рЭЬЗ(not рЭСО). For a closer look at the relationship of the diпђАerent probabilistic
logic programming languages we refer to [83,10,38].
Neuro-symbolic reasoning Neuro-symbolic reasoning, as for example in DeepProbLog [1], takes the idea of probabilistic reasoning
with programs one step further. Here, instead of assuming that the probabilities of rules and facts are given, they are determined by
a machine learning model that predicts the probability of a fact or rule for a given ground instantiation of it. Instead of having the
same probability of stress for each person

0.4 вИґвИґ stress(рЭСЛ) вИґ вИУ person(рЭСЛ),
we might have a neural predicate nnвИУstress(рЭРірЭСФрЭСТ, рЭРЄрЭСЪрЭСЭрЭСЩрЭСЬрЭС¶рЭСЪрЭСТрЭСЫрЭС°) that models the probability of a person being stressed based on age
and employment. In the DeepProbLog language, this would be expressed as

nnвИУstress(рЭРі, рЭРЄ) вИґвИґ stress вИґ вИУ person(рЭСЛ), age(рЭРі, рЭСЛ), employment(рЭРЄ, рЭСЛ).
We consider two tasks in neuro-symbolic reasoning that need to be solved.
(T1) Determine the probability of a query, given a neuro-symbolic program.
For DeepProbLog, this again corresponds to a query using an algebraic measure: when the machine learning model that predicts
the probabilities of facts and rules is пђБxed we can see DeepProbLog programs as ProbLog programs and, thus, evaluate probabilistic
queries using algebraic measures. Here, we can again use the probabilistic semiring оИЉ .
(T2) Train the model to predict the correct probabilities.
Usually, this is done using gradient descent to minimize some error function рЭСТрЭСЯрЭСЯ that depends on the output of the neural networks.
In order to use gradient descent, it is necessary to compute the derivative of рЭСТрЭСЯрЭСЯ. In the DeepProbLog setting, this can be rather
complicated, as рЭСТрЭСЯрЭСЯ is not limited to measuring whether the predicted value was correct. Instead, the error can also be determined
by the probability of a derived atom. E.g. in the smokers setting, we may have data about whether a person smokes but not about a
persons stress. Thus, applying gradient descent to the error function of the smokers model involves obtaining its derivative in terms
of the output of the neural network.
Manhaeve et al. [1] showed that we can compute this gradient by weighted model counting over the gradient semiring рЭРЇрЭСЕрЭРірЭРЈ.
Therefore, we can also model this task using an algebraic measure over

рЭРЇрЭСЕрЭРірЭРЈ = ([0, 1] √Ч вДЭ, вКХ, вКЧ, (0, 0), (1, 0)),
where

(рЭСЭ1 , рЭСС1 )вКХ(рЭСЭ2 , рЭСС2 ) = (рЭСЭ1 + рЭСЭ2 , рЭСС1 + рЭСС2 )
(рЭСЭ1 , рЭСС1 )вКЧ(рЭСЭ2 , рЭСС2 ) = (рЭСЭ1 рЭСЭ2 , рЭСС1 рЭСЭ2 + рЭСС2 рЭСЭ1 ).
Intuitively, the пђБrst entry corresponds to the probability, as before, whereas the second entry computes the derivative of the пђБrst
with respect to some parameter.
Knowledge compilation Using the AASC approach we may further obtain a tractable circuit representation of the program. This may
seem counterintuitive, since we said in the Introduction that we use knowledge compilation to perform AASC and not the other
way around. However, it shows that when we can do weighted model counting over semirings, then we can also do knowledge
compilation.
For knowledge compilation, we need a somewhat non-standard semiring similar to the provenance semirings of Green et al. [77].
Given a semiring оИЊ and a set of propositional atoms оЙВ , we deпђБne the knowledge compilation semiring of algebraic circuits over оЙВ
and оИЊ as

Here, an algebraic circuit over оЙВ and оИЊ is a directed acyclic graph (DAG), where each vertex is labeled by either +, вИЧ, a literal

either labeled by a literal or a semiring value and vertices with incoming edges are either labeled by + or вИЧ. Furthermore, there must

Clearly, using the standard equality in the sense of syntactical equality is inadequate, since оИЈоИѓ(оЙВ, оИЊ) is not commutative:

рЭЫЉ[рЭЬО]оИЊ = рЭЫљ[рЭЬО]оИЊ .
Here, рЭЫЉ[рЭЬО] denotes that we replace every literal рЭСЩ in рЭЫЉ by рЭЬО(рЭСЩ) its semiring value under рЭЬО . Then, рЭЫЉ[рЭЬО]оИЊ corresponds to evaluating

the circuit.
Note that this idea is not new: Kimmig et al. [29] introduced a BDD semiring, where conjunction and disjunction correspond to
the respective operation on BDDs, thus, allowing knowledge compilation to a BDD. However, in contrast to the BDD semiring, our
semiring allows any kind of algebraic circuit, even those that classically do not correspond to a tractable circuit class such as BDD,
SDD, or d-DNNF.
Apart from this, we may obtain syntactically diпђАerent results depending on how we evaluate a query over оИЈоИѓ(оЙВ, оИЊ). This,
however, is of no concern to us. We can show that given any such syntactic representation of the result, we can evaluate meaningful
queries.
Lemma 8 (Semiring Knowledge Compilation). Let рЭЬЗ = вЯ®ќ†, рЭЫЉ, оИЊвЯ© be a factorized algebraic measure with weight function рЭЫљ that factorizes рЭЬЗ .

where we write atoms in italics рЭС£ and semiring values in boldface рЭРѓ.

Thus, given any algebraic circuit рЭЫЊ that corresponds to the result of the knowledge compilation query рЭЬЗрЭРЊрЭРґ (ќ†), we can evaluate
any factorized measure query over оИЊ using рЭЫЊ in linearly many semiring operations in the size of рЭЫЊ . Thus, the key is to compute a
small circuit рЭЫЊ and the latter eпђГciently. It is well-known that such arithmetic circuits can easily be obtained from BDDs, SDDs, or
d-DNNFs [26], by replacing disjunctions and conjunctions by additions and multiplications, respectively.
For us the algebraic circuit semirings are not only of theoretical but also of practical interest. Namely, as we describe in more
detail later in the section on implementation, we use SHARP SAT-TD [47], which enables weighted model counting over semirings.
Thus, we can use it for knowledge compilation using the idea of the algebraic circuit semirings. This again shows the power of the
semiring paradigm. Since SHARP SAT-TD is implemented in such a manner that it works for general commutative semirings, we can
solve a variety of diпђАerent interesting tasks by performing a minor modiпђБcation. In fact, the adaptation of SHARP SAT-TD to enable
knowledge compilation to smooth d-DNNF was possible in less than 500 lines of code, including the parsing of additional input
arguments and the adaptation of the preprocessor to handle weights over general semirings. Details and an independent evaluation
can be found in the corresponding conference paper [84].
4.2. OptP-hard problems
The applications discussed above share the fact that they are Weighted Model Counting problems in the strict sense, i.e. they are
#P-hard. We thus expect that they are practically harder to solve using current implementations than other AASC problems which
are not #P-hard. For example, AASC over the Boolean semiring рЭФє corresponds to checking the consistency of an answer set program
and is therefore NP-complete. Arguably, it makes sense to consider such problems which are practically easier to solve separately.
11

Another class of problems that are aпђАected by this reasoning are optimization problems. There is a broad variety of extensions of
ASP that introduce diпђАerent ways to add preferences to programs [23,24]. We focus instead on the Most Probable Explanation (MPE)
task from the probabilistic logic programming literature [85], which also пђБts into the context of optimization problems.

output.

5. Solving AASC problems
The main goal of our work is to solve AASC problems more eпђГciently. In order to do so, we need to aggregate the values
associated with each answer set of a program by the weighted formula. Already computing one answer set is NP-hard and computing
an aggregate of over all of them can be even harder, depending on the chosen aggregate, which in our setting corresponds to the
addition of the semiring. There has, however, been a lot of research on diпђАerent approaches that can be used to overcome this
hardness in an eпђАort to provide an eпђГcient implementation [36,35,58,39,13]. While these approaches diпђАer signiпђБcantly in their
details, they usually follow a similar overall workпђВow.
In this section, we give a broad overview of both the shared workпђВow and the diпђАerences between the approach to arrive at a
solution. Especially, we investigate the conditions under which each of the approaches is likely to succeed. This is interesting, on the
one hand, from a user perspective, as it can allow one to make an informed choice for a tool, depending on the conditions satisпђБed
by the instances at hand. On the other hand, it is useful for us since we can select the most promising approach as the basis for our
work.
5.1. Overall workпђВow
A schema of the general pipeline that is usually shared between diпђАerent solvers is shown in Fig. 3. Many steps are optional
and diпђАerent solvers use diпђАerent paths through this pipeline. Usually, solvers accept possibly non-ground programs ќ† with some
additional annotations for the algebraic measure. Often, this program is then grounded, resulting in a propositional program, which
is simpliпђБed in the next step. Afterwards, the actual AASC starts, by converting the program into an equivalent tractable circuit
representation оИѓ via some form of knowledge compilation (KC). In the last step, the result is obtained by evaluation over the circuit оИѓ .
Before we go into more details, we would like to note that in some solvers these steps are intertwined instead of being executed one
after the other.
We focus on the knowledge compilation step. First, there has already been a lot of work on the other steps of the pipeline: Fierens
et al. [35] showed that for probabilistic inference with ProbLog programs, it is suпђГcient to restrict oneself to the relevant ground
part of the program, which can signiпђБcantly reduce the size of the problem. Tsamoura et al. [86] went even further and used the
magic set technique [87] to identify the relevant part during grounding, thus, eliminating the need to even produce the remaining
irrelevant part in this phase. Additionally, Shterionov [88, Chapter 3] thoroughly investigated a wide range of simpliпђБcations for
probabilistic logic programs that were shown to lead to signiпђБcantly improved solving times on many benchmark sets.

Second, we argue that the knowledge compilation step has the largest potential for improvement and is likely to lead to the
biggest eпђАect. Due to these reasons, we focus on the knowledge compilation step, as we assume its investigation to be the most
fruitful.
5.2. Knowledge compilation
The idea behind knowledge compilation is the following: while certain problems, such as AASC, are hard on general logical
theories, there are so called tractable circuit representations, where this is not the case. Here, we shortly summarize the diпђАerent
theoretical guarantees associated with compiling a given representation of programs to prominent circuit classes in addition to
implementations that support them. For a more in-depth introduction and discussion, we refer the interested reader to Appendix B.
A summary is given in Table 1. We see that compiling to MODS leads to a circuit of size linear in the number of answer sets.
Thus, this strategy is tempting, when there are вАЬfewвАЭ of them, which is however rarely the case for probabilistic logic programs,
where the number of answer sets grows exponentially in the number of probabilistic facts. For the compilation of CNFs and acyclic
programs as well as the explicit dynamic programming approach [21], we see that the guarantees strongly depend on the structural
parameters, i.e., treewidth or pathwidth of associated structures. Here, the guarantee for CNFs seems to be the most desirable, due to
the single exponential dependency on treewidth compared to the at least double exponential dependency for the other approaches.
It is, however, important to note that
) upper bounds, rather than lower bounds, and (2) Amarilli et al. [52] were
( (1) these are only
able to obtain an upper bound of оИї 25(рЭСШ+1) вЛЕ |Vars(рЭРґ)| by slightly loosening the requirements for SDDs. Therefore, we may obtain
much better performance in practice as the guarantees suggest.
For the direct compilation of programs to sd-DNNF as given by Aziz et al. [89], no guarantees are known. It is interesting, since
contrary to CNF and acyclic program compilation, here, the requirement for stability of models is taken care of during compilation
rather than beforehand. Taking care of it in advance during cycle breaking requires transformations that may increase the treewidth
and size of programs. Here, this is solved by adding so called loop formulas [90], which are clauses that prohibit cyclic derivations,
during compilation. However, loop formulas as introduced by Lin and Zhao [90] may span a whole SCC of the dependency graph of
the program.3 This can destroy previously low treewidth and be devastating for the compilation performance. Apart from that, not
only the treewidth may suпђАer: we may end up with exponentially many additional clauses in the size of the largest SCC [91]. This
version of D SHARP was removed from the ProbLog implementation, presumably for the above reasons. While there are strategies
to reduce the number of necessary loop formulas [92,93], their adaptation would not circumvent the exponential worst case lower
bound on the number of added clauses.
Summary Summing up, we see that there is a wide range of diпђАerent approaches to knowledge compilation or more generally solving
AASC problems, coming with diпђАerent theoretical guarantees. Mostly, the guarantees are exponential in a structural parameter
associated with the formula that is to be compiled. The only exception is the approach of the solvers LPMLN [10] and plingo [38]
that enumerate all the models.
The most promising theoretical results are known for the compilation of CNFs to sd-DNNFs. Furthermore, the recent work of
Korhonen and J√§rvisalo [47] showed that one can achieve performance improvements by using the latter explicitly. This begs the
question whether and how we can convert programs of low treewidth into CNFs of low treewidth, in order to fruitfully exploit these
recent advancements.
6. ClarkвАЩs completion
A crucial step in the translation of programs to CNF formulas is the well-known ClarkвАЩs Completion [54]. While it is deпђБned on
general programs, it is only guaranteed to lead to an equivalent CNF, when the program it was applied to does not have cycles in

3

They usually contain even more variables, however already this is devastating for knowledge compilation.
14

Artificial Intelligence 330 (2024) 104109

T. Eiter, M. Hecher and R. Kiesel

its positive dependence graph. The traditional, most well-known version of ClarkвАЩs Completion does not allow for bounds on the
treewidth of the resulting CNF in terms of the treewidth of the primal graph of the original program. There has, however, already
been work by Hecher that does imply such bounds. In the following, we пђБrst discuss the original version of ClarkвАЩs Completion and
its limitations as well as the modiпђБcations by Hecher. After that we show that even HecherвАЩs version allows for further improvements
and introduce an extended version for which we can obtain better bounds.
The idea behind ClarkвАЩs Completion is the following: if we have an atom рЭСО that is in the head of the rules рЭСЯ1 , вА¶ , рЭСЯрЭСЫ , then рЭСО is only

Recall that a graph of primal treewidth рЭСШ has incidence treewidth less or equal to рЭСШ + 1. Thus, in the worst case the guarantees
of Theorem 16 only diпђАer from those of Theorem 14 by a constant factor. However, since incidence treewidth may be arbitrarily
smaller than primal treewidth, the guarantees with respect to incidence treewidth can be much better. The latter can also be seen
in Example 11, where we consider a family of programs with constant incidence treewidth but unbounded primal treewidth. Thus,
Theorem 16 broadens the range of cases, where we can fruitfully use CNF-translation followed by algebraic model counting on the
CNF for algebraic answer set counting.
In order to solve AASC with knowledge compilation via a version of ClarkвАЩs Completion, we however пђБrst need to ensure that the
resulting CNF preserves the models of the original program bijectively.
7. Cycle breaking
Recall that ClarkвАЩs Completion is only guaranteed to capture the answer sets of a program if the program is acyclic. Thus, for a
program with a cyclic dependency graph, we пђБrst need to make some further eпђАort to ensure that ClarkвАЩs Completion will be correct.
This process is commonly referred to as cycle breaking and transforms a program ќ† into a program оИѓ(ќ†).
There has been a lot of work on cycle breaking both in the ASP community [59,57,55] for translations to SAT as well in the
probabilistic reasoning community [58] speciпђБcally for weighted model counting. The basic idea of cycle breaking is to modify the
program in such a way that afterwards the models of the Clark Completion and the modiпђБed program are the same. In the context of
general AASC, it is moreover important that the answer sets before and after cycle breaking are in a speciпђБc one-to-one relationship.
Formally, the cycle breaking needs to be faithful:
DeпђБnition 17 (Faithfulness). A cycle breaking оИѓ(.) is faithful (for ќ†), if:
(i) |оИ≠оИњ(ќ†)| = |оИ≠оИњ(оИѓ(ќ†))|, and
(ii) оИ≠оИњ(ќ†) = {оИµ вИ© оИ≠(ќ†) вИ£ оИµ вИИ оИ≠оИњ(оИѓ(ќ†))}.
As expected, faithfulness guarantees that we can perform AASC over the program obtained by cycle breaking without changing
the result.
Lemma 18 (Faithfulness Implies Query Invariance). Let рЭЬЗ = вЯ®ќ†, рЭЫЉ, оИЊвЯ© be a measure and let оИѓ(.) be a faithful cycle breaking for ќ†. Then,
for рЭЬЗ вА≤ = вЯ®оИѓ(ќ†), рЭЫЉ, оИЊвЯ© it holds that рЭЬЗ(рЭСО) = рЭЬЗ вА≤ (рЭСО) for every рЭСО вИИ оИ≠(ќ†).
The translations of Lin and Zhao [59] and Hecher [55] do not satisfy this requirement for all program, as they serve to check the
existence of an answer set.
In contrast, the two cycle breakings of Janhunen and Niemel√§ [56] and Mantadelis and Janssens [58] are faithful for all programs. In fact, both have been considered in the context of probabilistic reasoning. Janhunen and Niemel√§вАЩs was considered for the
implementation of Problog (cf. [36]) and Mantadelis and JanssensвАЩs is still part of the standard Problog implementation.
In this section, our aim is to пђБnd a cycle breaking that will result in good AASC-performance. As mentioned before, our main
criterion here is whether we can bound the increase of the treewidth. However, of course also the increase in program size and
insights regarding the semantic complexity are relevant for us. In the following, we thus пђБrst analyze the state of the art of faithful
cycle breakings. Afterwards we introduce a novel cycle breaking called TоИЉ -Unfolding, which we use in our implementation, and
argue that it has favorable properties.
7.1. Necessity of cycle breaking
Before we consider existing cycle breakings, we пђБrst motivate the need for it by giving an example program, where the models of
ClarkвАЩs Completion do not align with the stable models.
Example 12. Consider again the program ќ†рЭС†рЭСЪ from the running example.

{st(1)} вЖР

{st(2)} вЖР

{st(3)} вЖР

{inf(3, 1)} вЖР

{inf(1, 2)} вЖР

{inf (2, 3)} вЖР

sm(1) вЖР st(1)

sm(1) вЖР inf(3, 1), sm(3)

sm(2) вЖР st(2)

Recall that {рЭСО} вЖР is a shorthand for the two rules рЭСО вЖР not рЭСЫрЭСО and рЭСЫрЭСО вЖР not рЭСО. Thus, each of the stress and inпђВuence atoms contribute

Without any additional constraints, this means that we can choose the truth values of st(рЭСЦ) and inf(рЭСЦ, рЭСЧ) arbitrarily as long we assign

{sm(1), sm(2), sm(3), nst(1), nst(2), nst(3), inf (3, 1), inf (1, 2), inf (2, 3)}.

{nst(1), nst(2), nst(3), inf (3, 1), inf (1, 2), inf (2, 3)}
is also a model of ќ†рЭС†рЭСЪ .
The previous example clearly shows that the problem with ClarkвАЩs Completion on general problem is caused by cyclic derivations.
Cyclic derivations are those that use that an atom рЭСО is true to derive that рЭСО is true.
7.2. The MJ(.) cycle breaking [58]
The strategy of Mantadelis and JanssensвАЩs [58] cycle breaking MJ(.) to avoid such cycвАЩlic derivations is to introduce copies рЭСОрЭРє of
an atom рЭСО for рЭРє вКЖ оИ≠(ќ†) that intuitively capture derivations of рЭСО that do not positively use any atom from рЭРє . Then, we can use the
atom рЭСО{рЭСП} in a derivation of рЭСП from рЭСО, even if рЭСО and рЭСП are in a cyclic dependency. Naturally, introducing a copy рЭСОрЭРє for every subset
рЭРє вКЖ оИ≠(ќ†) is undesirable as it would lead to an exponential number of atoms.
In order to avoid this whenever possible, the cycle breaking MJ(.) makes use of two ideas. Firstly, it uses the fact that sometimes
the derivations for рЭСО that do not use atoms from рЭРє or рЭРє вА≤ align. Then, it does not make sense to introduce two separate atoms рЭСОрЭРє , рЭСОрЭРє вА≤
and one can use one of them for both cases. Second, if every derivation for рЭСП uses the atoms in рЭРє вКЖ оИ≠(ќ†) before using the atom рЭСО,
then no atom рЭСО{рЭСП} will be created but only an atom рЭСОрЭРє вИ™{рЭСП} .
We illustrate the idea behind MJ(.) on our running example.
Example 13 (cont.). First consider the rules for the guesses of the stressed and inпђВuences predicate.

{st(1)} вЖР

{st(2)} вЖР

{st(3)} вЖР

{inf (3, 1)} вЖР

{inf(1, 2)} вЖР

{inf(2, 3)} вЖР

Here, we do not need to change anything, since these atoms are not involved in any cycles. Next, we consider the remaining atoms

Here, sm(1), sm(2) and sm(3) are in a cyclic dependency. We пђБrst consider sm(1). Due to the cycle, we cannot use all derivations of

sm(1) вЖР inf(3, 1), sm(3).

without {sm(2)} and without {sm(3), sm(2)} diпђАer (and similarly for the derivations of sm(2), sm(3)).
This brings us to the question of how the treewidth changes when we apply this cycle breaking. The strategy in Theorem 16 was
intuitively to add every copy of an atom to every bag in a tree decomposition that contains that atom. If we do the same with every
copy рЭСОрЭРє for an atom рЭСО and рЭРє вКЖ оИ≠(ќ†), we only get an upper-bound that is exponential in the number of atoms of ќ†. We may do
slightly but not signiпђБcantly better as the following two theorems show.
We пђБrst recall that a simple cycle in a directed graph рЭРЇ is a (directed) path in рЭРЇ from a vertex рЭС£ вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) to рЭС£ such that the only

7.3. The JN(.) cycle breaking [56]
We continue with the second cycle breaking JN(.) due to Janhunen and Niemel√§ [56]. The previous strategy was to capture partial
derivations explicitly by introducing auxiliary atoms leading to an acyclic and answer set preserving program. The cycle breaking
JN(.) introduced in this work instead leaves the original program untouched but adds constraints that ensure each true atom can be
derived. For this, it makes use of level rankings [95]. Intuitively, the levels of atoms specify in which order they can be used in a
derivation, i.e. when the level of atom рЭСО is lower than that of atom рЭСП, then we can use a rule that has рЭСП in the head and рЭСО in the body
to derive рЭСП. It was shown that it is possible to characterize the answer sets of a program in terms of ClarkвАЩs Completion in combination
with level ranking constraints. Furthermore, by adding constraints on the level ranking we obtain a one-to-one correspondence [95].
We illustrate the idea behind JN (.) on our running example and refer the interested reader to [56] for details.
Example 14 (cont.). As already mentioned, we keep the original set of rules untouched. That is JN(ќ†) = ќ† вИ™ ќ†вА≤ , where ќ†вА≤ contains
additional rules that specify level ranking constraints.

additional rules. More generally, atoms рЭСО that can only be derived from rules whose positive body atoms are not in a cycle with рЭСО do
not require level ranking constraints.
Thus, we only need to take care of the following rules:

sm(3) вЖР st(3)

sm(3) вЖР inf (2, 3), sm(2)

Theorem 21. Let ќ† be a normal answer set program of treewidth рЭСШ such that the largest strongly connected component of DEP(ќ†) has size
рЭС†. Then, the treewidth of JN(ќ†) is in оИї(рЭСШ2 + рЭСШ log2 (рЭС†)).
As with Theorem 16 the idea here is to take an existing tree decomposition of optimal width рЭСШ and add to each bag all auxiliary
atoms that are related to the atoms in the bag.
We point out that the quadratic dependency on the treewidth рЭСШ can actually be avoided by modifying Janhunen and Niemel√§вАЩs
deпђБnition of cycle breaking. For this, observe that the quadratic dependency stems from the inclusion of рЭР•рЭР≠(рЭСП, рЭСО) and рЭРђрЭРЃрЭРЬрЭРЬ(рЭСП, рЭСО) for
every рЭСО, рЭСП вИИ рЭЬТ(рЭС°) in the new bag рЭЬТ вА≤ (рЭС°). If we instead deпђБne one atom рЭР•рЭР≠(рЭСП, рЭСО, рЭСЯ) and рЭРђрЭРЃрЭРЬрЭРЬ(рЭСП, рЭСО, рЭСЯ) per rule рЭСЯ such that вДОрЭСТрЭСОрЭСС(рЭСЯ) = рЭСО and
рЭСП вИИ рЭСПрЭСЬрЭССрЭС¶(рЭСЯ), we do not need to include all such atoms in the same bag but can handle them in diпђАerent bags. It follows that there is
a cycle breaking with a treewidth upper bound in оИї(рЭСШ log2 (рЭС†)). In fact, we observed that the implementation of JN(.) in LP 2 LP 2 does
exactly this, meaning that it comes with a treewidth upper bound of оИї(рЭСШ log2 (рЭС†)).
We see that JN(.) gives better guarantees than MJ(.), since рЭСШ log2 (рЭС†) grows slower asymptotically than рЭСШ вЛЕ рЭС† вЛЕ рЭСР even when рЭСР = 1.
However, it uses a binary counter for the encoding of the level ranking. While this provides very good asymptotic size guarantees,
both in terms of the resulting program and its treewidth, binary encodings can sometimes have negative eпђАects on the practical
performance of SAT solvers [96] and can result in much slower performance than encodings of a larger asymptotic size.
7.4. TоИЉ -unfolding
We have seen that the current cycle breakings either come with weak upper bounds on the treewidth or have good upper bounds
but use an encoding that can lead to impairments of the practical performance of SAT solvers and therefore also of knowledge
compilers.
We want to avoid both drawbacks as far as possible. For this, we take a closer look at dependency graphs of programs and
introduce cbs(рЭРЇ) a novel parameter, the component-boosted backdoor size of a digraph. Intuitively, cbs(.) (deпђБned later in DeпђБnition 27)
provides a more пђБne grained measure of directed cyclicity. More importantly, we can exploit it in TоИЉ -Unfolding, our new cycle
breaking, which leads to the following guarantees:
Theorem 22. For any factorized measure рЭЬЗ = вЯ®ќ†, рЭЫЉ, оИЊвЯ©, we can construct in polynomial time in the size of ќ† given access to an NP-oracle
a factorized measure рЭЬЗ вА≤ = вЯ®ќ†вА≤ , рЭЫЉ, оИЊвЯ© with an acyclic program ќ†вА≤ such that
(i) for all рЭСО вИИ оИ≠(ќ†) it holds that рЭЬЗ(рЭСО) = рЭЬЗ вА≤ (рЭСО),
(ii) the treewidth of ќ†вА≤ is at most рЭСШ вЛЕ cbs(DEP(ќ†)), where рЭСШ is the treewidth of ќ†, and
(iii) the size of ќ†вА≤ is at most cbs(DEP(ќ†)) вЛЕ |ќ†|.
Here, the increase of the treewidth and the program is bounded by cbs(DEP(ќ†)). Thus, this factor only depends on the dependency
graph of ќ† and its cyclicity, rather than the number of atoms in the program or the size of the largest SCC of cbs(DEP(ќ†)).
As the name says, TоИЉ -Unfolding is related to another approach for AASC called TоИЉ -compilation [39]. Intuitively, the idea of
TоИЉ -compilation is to capture the derivations of atoms by iteratively compiling SDDs that are increasingly precise approximations
thereof. For this, we take at each step all rules рЭСЯ1 , вА¶ , рЭСЯрЭСЪ in ќ† such that вДОрЭСТрЭСОрЭСС(рЭСЯрЭСЦ ) = рЭСО for some atom рЭСО вИИ оИ≠(ќ†). Then, we пђБrst conjoin
the SDDs that represent the derivations of the body atoms of each rule рЭСЯрЭСЦ . By disjoining the resulting SDDs for each rule рЭСЯрЭСЦ , we get a
better approximation of the derivations of рЭСО.

TоИЉ -Unfolding, which takes as input a program ќ† and an unfolding sequence рЭС† вИИ оИ≠(ќ†)вИЧ , which is a list of atoms рЭС†1 вА¶ рЭС†рЭСЫ , with

does not positively depend on any variable and can thus be considered once before all other variables and never again afterwards.

This theorem gives us a suпђГcient condition for an unfolding sequence рЭС† to lead to faithfulness of TоИЉ -U NFOLD (., рЭС†) with respect to

We remark that the converse of this Lemma does not hold.
There is another relevant observation that we need to keep in mind when it comes to knowledge compilation later on. While the
guarantee generally only gives us an exponential upper bound in terms of treewidth, we actually know that this upper bound is too
pessimistic. Consider the following scenario: if we introduce рЭСЪ copies рЭСО, рЭСО1 , вА¶ , рЭСОрЭСЪвИТ1 of a variable рЭСО using TоИЉ -Unfolding, then we
have a monotonic relation between the truth values of the copies. That is, if рЭСОрЭСЦ is true in a stable model оИµ of the program and рЭСЧ > рЭСЦ,
then also рЭСОрЭСЧ must be true in оИµ . Thus, restricted to the variables рЭСО, рЭСО1 , вА¶ , рЭСОрЭСЪвИТ1 , there are actually at most рЭСЪ diпђАerent assignments that
can be extended to a stable model. It follows that for the variables in a bag рЭЬТ вА≤ (рЭС°) of the tree decomposition that we constructed in
the proof of Lemma 26 only рЭСЪрЭСШ = 2рЭСШвЛЕlog2 (рЭСЪ) diпђАerent assignments can be extended to a stable model. We thus believe that knowledge
compilation has better performance than Theorem 36 guarantees.
Motivated by Lemma 26 and Theorem 25, we say that an unfolding sequence рЭС† is a path-preserving рЭСЪ-unfolding sequence (for
рЭСР
рЭСР
digraph рЭРЇ ), if for every simple (directed) path рЭЬЛ = (рЭСО1 , вА¶ , рЭСОрЭСЫ ) in рЭРЇ there is a (directed) path рЭЬЛрЭСР = (рЭСО11 , вА¶ , рЭСОрЭСЫрЭСЫ ) in UF(рЭРЇ, рЭС†) and every
variable рЭСО вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) occurs at most рЭСЪ times in рЭС†. Naturally, we are interested in path-preserving рЭСЪ-unfolding sequences for small рЭСЪ.
Since also Lemma 26 does not access the actual program itself but only its dependency graph and the unfolding sequence, we can
abstract away the program and search for path-preserving рЭСЪ unfolding sequences on digraphs directly. As we do not want to do a
blind search, we instead consider backdoors [60] and generalize them to component-boosted backdoors into acyclicity because we can
give guarantees for the maximum necessary рЭСЪ based on them. Backdoors have already been considered in the context of ASP [60].
Usually, the notion of a backdoor for a digraph рЭРЇ is a vertex set рЭСЖ , such that рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ satisпђБes some desirable property. For us this
property is (directed or undirected) acyclicity of рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ , since we can пђБnd a suitable unfolding sequence when given рЭСЖ in either case.
Both the backdoor size of a digraph and its generalization to component-boosted backdoor size intuitively measure the cyclicity
of the given digraph by asking how many vertices need to be вАЬcut outвАЭ in order for the remaining digraph to be (almost) acyclic.
When a parameter value is low, an unfolding sequence рЭС† exists such that TоИЉ -U NFOLD (., рЭС†) is faithful and every variable occurs only
a few times in рЭС†.
DeпђБnition 27 (Backdoor, Component-boosted Backdoor). Let рЭРЇ be a digraph. Then, bs(рЭРЇ), the backdoor size of рЭРЇ is

min{|рЭСЖ| вИ£ рЭСЖ вКЖ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ), рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ is a polyforest or acyclic},
where a polyforest is graph that has no undirected cycles.
Furthermore, cbs(рЭРЇ), the component-boosted backdoor size of рЭРЇ , is
(i) 1, if рЭРЇ is acyclic (which includes рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) = вИЕ),
(ii) 2, if рЭРЇ is a polytree, i.e. a connected polyforest,
(iii) max{cbs(рЭРґ) вИ£ рЭРґ вИИ SCC(рЭРЇ)}, if рЭРЇ is cyclic but not strongly connected,
(iv) min{cbs(рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ) вЛЕ (|рЭСЖ| + 1) вИ£ рЭСЖ вКЖ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ), рЭСЖ вЙ† вИЕ} otherwise.
As the name suggests, рЭСРрЭСПрЭС† adds component-boosting to a speciпђБc variant of backdoors. A related parameter is elimination distance [98]. However, for elimination distance the removal set рЭСЖ in (iv) may only contain 1 element and results in cost cbs(рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ) + 1.
Thus, the elimination distance to acyclic digraphs and polytrees is bounded iпђА cbs(.) is bounded, but na√ѓvely, we can only assert that
if the elimination distance is рЭСШ then cbs(.) is less or equal to 2рЭСШ .
The main diпђАerence compared to the backdoor size is that component-boosted backdoor size additionally takes into account that
рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ may consist of separate SCCs that can be handled recursively. We can therefore bound cbs(.) in terms of bs(.) as follows:
Lemma 28. Let рЭРЇ be a digraph. Then, cbs(рЭРЇ) вЙ§ 2 вЛЕ (bs(рЭРЇ) + 1).
Proof. If рЭРЇ is acyclic, then

cbs(рЭРЇ) = 1 вЙ§ 2 = 2 вЛЕ (0 + 1).
If рЭРЇ is a polytree, then

cbs(рЭРЇ) = 2 вЙ§ 2 = 2 вЛЕ (0 + 1).
If рЭРЇ is cyclic and strongly connected, let рЭСЖ вИЧ be a set of vertices such that |рЭСЖ вИЧ | = bs(рЭРЇ) and рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ вИЧ is a polyforest or acyclic. Then

cbs(рЭРЇ) = min{cbs(рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ) вЛЕ (|рЭСЖ| + 1) вИ£ рЭСЖ вКЖ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ), рЭСЖ вЙ† вИЕ}
вЙ§ cbs(рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ вИЧ ) вЛЕ (|рЭСЖ вИЧ | + 1)
вЙ§ 2 вЛЕ (bs(рЭРЇ) + 1).
If рЭРЇ is cyclic but not strongly connected, then

cbs(рЭРЇ) = max{cbs(рЭРґ) вИ£ рЭРґ вИИ SCC(рЭРЇ)}.

Example 17 (contвАЩd). Consider the dependency graph DEP(ќ†рЭС†рЭСЪ ) in Fig. 11a. It is strongly connected and not a polytree, therefore

We see that if we take away any рЭСЖрЭСЦ = {sm(рЭСЦ)}, рЭСЦ = 1, вА¶ , 3, then DEP(ќ†рЭС†рЭСЪ ) вІµ рЭСЖрЭСЦ is acyclic. It follows that cbs(DEP(ќ†рЭС†рЭСЪ )) вЙ§
cbs(DEP(ќ†рЭС†рЭСЪ ) вІµ рЭСЖрЭСЦ ) вЛЕ (|рЭСЖрЭСЦ | + 1) = 2. Since we need to remove at least one element, this is also a lower bound and hence
cbs(DEP(ќ†рЭС†рЭСЪ )) = 2.
In this example the upper bound given by the backdoor size and component-boosted backdoor size align. However, for larger,
more complex graphs рЭСРрЭСПрЭС†(.) can be much smaller than the upper bound guaranteed by backdoor size.
With the deпђБnition of the component-boosted backdoor size in mind, we state the main result of this section.
Theorem 22. For any factorized measure рЭЬЗ = вЯ®ќ†, рЭЫЉ, оИЊвЯ©, we can construct in polynomial time in the size of ќ† given access to an NP-oracle
a factorized measure рЭЬЗ вА≤ = вЯ®ќ†вА≤ , рЭЫЉ, оИЊвЯ© with an acyclic program ќ†вА≤ such that
(i) for all рЭСО вИИ оИ≠(ќ†) it holds that рЭЬЗ(рЭСО) = рЭЬЗ вА≤ (рЭСО),
(ii) the treewidth of ќ†вА≤ is at most рЭСШ вЛЕ cbs(DEP(ќ†)), where рЭСШ is the treewidth of ќ†, and
(iii) the size of ќ†вА≤ is at most cbs(DEP(ќ†)) вЛЕ |ќ†|.
Of course it is somewhat undesirable that we need access to an NP-oracle for the construction. However, this is unavoidable,
since computing cbs(.) is NP-hard.
Theorem 29. The problem of checking whether cbs(рЭРЇ) вЙ§ рЭСШ given a digraph рЭРЇ and рЭСШ вИИ вДХ in the input is NP-complete.
Proof (Sketch). NP-membership is easy to see by a guess and check algorithm.
For NP-hardness, we use a reduction from SAT by constructing a digraph рЭРЇ such that its backdoor size is рЭСШ iпђА the SAT instance
is solvable. Then, we modify the digraph in such a manner that backdoor size and component-boosted backdoor size are equal to
obtain the desired result. вЦ°
We will see later on that luckily the NP-hardness does not cause any problems in practice.
To prove Theorem 22, we show that every digraph рЭРЇ has some path-preserving cbs(рЭРЇ)-unfolding sequence, using structural
induction on the deпђБnition of cbs(.).
Lemma 30. Let рЭРЇ be an acyclic digraph. Then, there exists a path-preserving 1-unfolding sequence. For case (i), we obtain:
Proof. Let рЭС† be an unfolding sequence where every рЭСО вИИ рЭСЙ (рЭРЇ) occurs exactly once and which obeys a topological ordering of рЭРЇ .
Then, UF(рЭРЇ, рЭС†) is equal to рЭРЇ (modulo variable renaming) and therefore path-preserving. вЦ°
Next, we consider case (ii), where рЭРЇ is a polytree.

handle рЭСЖ and рЭРЇ вІµ рЭСЖ separately.
